### Case Study 3 (Year: 2020)

Parmal is a small but underdeveloped district. It has a rocky terrain that is not suitable for agriculture, though some subsistence agriculture is being done on small plots of land. The area receives adequate rainfall and has an irrigation canal flowing through it. Amaria, its administrative centre, is a medium-sized town. It houses a large district hospital, an Industrial Training Institute and some privately owned skill training centres. It has all the facilities of a district headquarters. A trunk railway line passes approximately 50 kilometers from Amaria. Its poor connectivity is a major reason for the absence of any major industry therein. The state government offers a 10 year tax holiday as an incentive to new industry. In 2010 Anil, an industrialist decided to take benefits to set up Amaria Plastic Works (APW) in Noora village, about 20km from Amaria. While the factory was being built, Anil hired the required key labour and got them trained at the skill training centres at Amria. This act of his made the key personnel very loyal to APW. APW started production in 2011 with the labour drawn fully from Noora village. The villagers were very happy to get employment near their homes and were motivated by the key personnel to meet the production targets with high quality. APW started making large profits, a sizable portion of which was used to improve the quality of life in Noora. By 2016, Noora could boast of a greener village and a renovated village temple. Anil liaised with the local MLA to increase the frequency of the bus services to Amria. The government also opened a primary health care centre and primary school at Noora in buildings constructed by APW. APW used its CSR funds to set up women’s self-help groups, subsidize primary education to the village children and procure an ambulance for use by its employed and the needy. In 2019, there was a minor fire in APW. It was quickly extinguished as fire safety protocols were in place in the factory. Investigations revealed that the factory had been using electricity in excess of its authorized capacity. This was soon rectified. The next year, due to nationwide lockdown, the requirement of production fell for four months. Anil decided that all employees would be paid regularly. He employed them to plant trees and improve the village habitat. APW had developed a reputation of high-quality production and a motivated workforce.

**a. Critically analyse the story of APW and state the ethical issues involved.

**b. Do you consider APW as a role model for the development of backward areas? Give reasons.
The case of **Amaria Plastic Works (APW) illustrates how **private enterprise can act as a catalyst for development in a **backward, low-connectivity district.
**Ethical positives in the story of APW

1. Inclusive Employment Generation - Local recruitment reduced migration and ensured **livelihood security.

2. Human Capital Development - Investment in **skill training enhanced employability and dignity of labour.

3. Stakeholder-Oriented Business Ethics - Profit-sharing through social investment reflects **stakeholder capitalism.

4. Ethical use of CSR beyond tokenism- SHGs, education subsidy, ambulance, habitat improvement

5. Virtue ethics - Ethical leadership during lockdown by prioritising **human well-being over profit.

6. Rule compliance - Fire incident managed due to **prior safety compliance.

7. Public-Private Collaboration - Constructive engagement with the state for connectivity, PHC, school support.
**Ethical issues (critical analysis)

1. Excess electricity usage reflects **procedural impropriety, even if later corrected.

2. Dependence on a Single Employer creates **economic vulnerability.

3. Plastic manufacturing inherently raises **long-term ecological concerns

4. Risk of **substituting state obligation with corporate benevolence (Philanthropy vs Rights) - Welfare delivered through CSR rather than institutional entitlements.

5. Unequal Spatial Development - Benefits concentrated in Noora, rest of Parmal district remains underdeveloped.

6. Paternalism: The village’s heavy reliance on Anil for healthcare, education, and transport creates a &quot;Company Town&quot; dynamic where the benefactor has disproportionate power.

7. Cronyism Risks: Liaising with an MLA for specific bus routes might raise questions of whether public resources were diverted to benefit a private entity's logistics.

b) **Yes, APW can be considered as a role model for rural development. Despite the minor regulatory lapse, its blueprint offers a sustainable path for &quot;backward&quot; regions.
**Reasons

1. Adherence to principle of Triple Bottom Line - people, profit and planet

2. Inclusive Growth: integrating tribal or rural populations into high-tech manufacturing through training.

3. Ethical Entrepreneurship - Profit combined with **social conscience.

4. Crisis Ethics - Lockdown response exemplifies **compassionate capitalism.

5. Trust-Based Industrial Relations - Loyalty built through **investment in people, not coercion.

6. Demonstration Effect - Shows how **industry can thrive even in low-connectivity regions.

7. Social Capital Building: It revitalized community spaces (temples, schools), fostering social cohesion.

8. PPP (Public-Private Partnership) at Grassroots

9. Market-Linked Skill Development: It utilized existing local institutions (ITIs) to create industry-ready talent.

10. Health as a Priority: The provision of an ambulance and a primary health center addressed the most critical vulnerability of rural life.

11. Financial Independence for Women: Through SHGs, it diversified the village's income sources beyond the factory floor.

12. Altruistic Management: Anil’s &quot;Loyalty First&quot; approach is a model for modern HR practices in volatile markets.
The story of APW is a triumph of **&quot;Profits with Purpose.&quot; Anil proved that with local capacity building and ethical treatment of labor, social and economic progress can be achieved in backward districts.
**

---

### Case Study 4 (Year: 2020)

The Chairman of Bharat Missiles Ltd (BML) was watching a program on TV wherein the Prime Minister was addressing the nation on the necessity of developing a self-reliant India. He subconsciously nodded in agreement and smiled to himself as he mentally reviewed BML’s journey in the past two decades. BML had admirably progressed from producing first-generation anti-tank guided missiles (ATGMs) to designing and producing state of the art ATGM weapon systems that would be the envy of any army. He sighed in reconciliation with his assumptions that the government would probably not alter the status quo of a ban on the export of military weaponry. To his surprise, the very next day he got a telephone call from the Director-General, Ministry of Defence, asking him to discuss the modalities of increasing BML production of ATGMs as there is a probability of exporting the same to a friendly country. The Director-General wanted the Chairman to discuss the details with his staff in Delhi next week. Two days later, at a press conference, the Defence Minister stated that he aims to double the current weapons export levels within five years. This would give an impetus to financing the development and manufacturing of indigenous weapons in the country. He also stated that all indigenous arms manufacturing nations have a very good record of the international arms trade. As the Chairman of BML, what are your views on the following points?

**a. As an arms exporter of a responsible nation like India, what are the ethical issues involved in arms trade?

**b. List five ethical factors that would influence the decision to sell arms to foreign governments.
&quot;A nation that stands for peace must also be prepared to protect it. However, a nation that profits from
the tools of war must carry the heavy burden of conscience.&quot;
As the Chairman of **BML, I see the government's shift toward arms exports as a double-edged sword. While it fulfills the vision of **Atmanirbhar Bharat, it also transitions BML to a global weapon-supplier.
**Ethical issues involved in arms trade by a responsible nation like India

1. Right to Life vs National Interest - Weapons enhance security but also possess inherent **lethality, raising moral concerns over loss of civilian life.

2. Just War Ethics - Risk of arms being used for **unjust aggression, repression, or violation of humanitarian law. Eg- Genocide in Rawanda

3. End-use dilemma - Difficulty in ensuring weapons are used **only for legitimate defence purposes.

4. Regional Destabilisation - Arms exports can **fuel arms races and escalate regional conflicts.

5. Moral Complicity - Exporting state may be ethically implicated in atrocities committed using supplied weapons.

6. Diversion Risk - Possibility of weapons falling into **non-state actors or terrorist groups. Eg-US-origin arms captured from Iraqi forces later used by **ISIS

7. Profit vs Peace - Tension between **economic gains and **ethical responsibility.

8. Dual-Use Technology Concerns: sensitive technology can be reverse-engineered or adapted for offensive purposes beyond the original agreement.

9. Global Image and Credibility - India’s identity as a **peace-oriented democracy may be affected by reckless exports.

10. Accountability and Transparency - Arms trade is often opaque, raising concerns of **corruption and lack of oversight. Eg- Bofors scam

11. If the regime is overthrown by a hostile or radical group, arms may fall into wrong hands.
**Ethical Factors Influencing Decision to Sell Arms to Foreign Governments (5 Factors)

1. Human Rights Record of the Recipient State - Arms should not be sold to regimes with a history of **systematic human rights violations. Eg- Eg- Germany suspended arms exports to **Saudi Arabia after the Jamal Khashoggi killing.

2. Purpose and End-Use Assurance - Clear commitment that weapons will be used **only for defensive and legitimate security needs.

3. Impact on Regional Peace - Exports must not **exacerbate existing conflicts or destabilise regions. Eg- no sale to Ukraine or Palestine

4. Compliance with International Law - The sale must comply with international arms control regimes (such as the **Wassenaar Arrangement or the **Arms Trade Treaty)

5. Alignment with India’s Strategic and Ethical Values - Sale should reinforce India’s image as a **responsible, rule-based, peace-oriented power.
While doubling exports is a valid economic goal, it must be governed by a **National Export Ethics Code. As Chairman of BML, I would support exports only when they align with **national security, global peace, and India’s civilisational ethos of ethical power.
**

---

### Case Study 5 (Year: 2020)

Rajesh Kumar is a senior public servant, with a reputation of honesty and forthrightness, currently posted in the Finance Ministry as the Head of the Budget Division. His department is presently busy in organizing the budgetary support to the states, four of which are due to the polls within the financial year. This year’s annual budget had allotted Rs 8300 crores for the National Housing Scheme (NHS), a centrally sponsored social housing scheme for the weaker sections of society. Rs 775 crores have been drawn for the NHS till June. The Ministry of Commerce had long been pursuing a case for setting up a Special Economic Zone (SEZ) in a southern state to boost exports. After two years of detailed discussion between the center and state, the Union Cabinet approved the project in August. The process was initiated to acquire the necessary land. Eighteen months ago a leading Public Sector Unit (PSU) had projected the need for setting up a large natural gas processing plant in a northern state for the regional gas grid. The land is already in possession of the PSU. The gas grid is an essential component of the national energy security strategy. After three rounds of global bidding, the project was allotted to an MNC, M/s XYZ Hydrocarbons. The first tranche of payment to the MNC is scheduled to be made in December. The Finance Ministry was asked for a timely allocation of an additional Rs 6000 crores for these two developmental projects. It was decided to recommend re-appropriation of this entire amount from NHS allocation. The file was forwarded to the Budget Department for their comments and further processing. On studying the case file, Rajesh Kumar realized that this re-appropriation may cause an inordinate delay in the execution of NHS, a project much publicized in the rallies of senior politicians. Correspondingly, the non-availability of finances would cause a financial loss in the SEZ and national embarrassment due to delayed payment in the international project. Rajesh Kumar discussed the matter with seniors. He was conveyed that this politically sensitive situation needs to be processed immediately. Rajesh Kumar realized that diversion of funds from the NHS could raise difficult questions for the government in the Parliament. Discuss the following with reference to this case-a. **Ethical issues involved in re-appropriation of funds from a welfare project to the development projects.

**b. Given the need for proper utilization of public funds, discuss the options available to Rajesh Kumar.

**c. Is resigning a worthy option?
As the Head of the Budget Division, Rajesh Kumar is at the intersection of **distributive justice (housing for the poor) and **national strategic interest (energy security and exports).
**Guiding Principle- Utilitarian Ethics - Maximising overall public good while minimising harm.
**Ethical Issues Involved in Re-appropriation of Funds

1. From a Utilitarian **perspective, the gas grid secures energy for millions. However, from a **Rights-based perspective, homeless have a prior claim to the funds specifically marked for them.

2. Equity vs Efficiency - Diverting funds from **housing for the poor to capital-intensive projects prioritizes efficiency over **social justice.

3. Violation of Welfare Commitment - NHS was approved by Parliament and publicly promised, creating an ethical obligation of **policy fidelity.

4. Impact on Vulnerable Sections - Delay in NHS disproportionately affects **economically weaker sections, violating **distributive justice.

5. Parliamentary Accountability - Re-appropriation without transparent disclosure may undermine **legislative oversight.

6. Pressure to act swiftly due to elections risks **politicisation of financial decisions.

7. Opportunity Cost Ethics - Using welfare funds for development projects raises questions on **prioritisation of scarce resources.

8. International Credibility - Delay in payment to the MNC risks **national embarrassment and reputational damage.

9. Right vs Right dilemma - choosing between **social justice or long-term strategic interests.

10. Administrative Integrity - Challenge in balancing **obedience to authority with **constitutional
**morality.

11. Any perception of arbitrary diversion can erode **citizens’ trust in governance.
**Options Available to Rajesh Kumar

**1. Process the Re-appropriation as directed as energy security and India’s reputation is at stake.



**2. Partial Re-appropriation: Take only a small portion from the NHS that is unlikely to be spent in the current quarter.

**3. Formal Dissent - record a detailed &quot;Note on File&quot; highlighting the impact on NHS

**4. Explore Alternative Fiscal Options -

a. Propose **supplementary grants, **contingency fund use for payment to MNC

b. Market borrowings or
PPP for PSU project

c. Using **crowdfunding or CSR funds for NHS scheme by creating PM Housing Fund similar to
PM-CARES fund
**Resignation as an option
**No, resignation is not the most ethical option

1. Abdication of Responsibility - highlights lack of courage of conviction in Rajesh as he adopts escapist route

2. Loss of Institutional Corrective Voice - Remaining in office allows Rajesh to **influence outcomes ethically.

3. Doctrine of Commitment - Civil servants are expected to **resolve dilemmas within the system, not exit at the first conflict.

4. Availability of Ethical Alternatives - Since lawful and ethical options exist, resignation is **disproportionate.

5. Public Interest Supremacy - Ethical action lies in **harm minimisation, not moral withdrawal.

6. Resignation is justified only if asked to do something **illegal, unconstitutional, or blatantly unethical.

7. Harm minimisation principle - Staying allows Rajesh Kumar to **reduce damage to NHS beneficiaries.
Rajesh Kumar’s ethical responsibility lies in **balancing priorities transparently, safeguarding the vulnerable, and upholding national commitments. **True integrity in public service lies in principled decision-making, not principled exit.
**

---

### Case Study 6 (Year: 2020)

You are a municipal commissioner of a large city, having the reputation of a very honest and upright officer. A huge multipurpose mall is under construction in your city in which a large number of daily wage earners are employed. One night, during monsoons, a big chunk of the roof collapsed causing instant death of four labourers including two minors. Many more were seriously injured requiring immediate medical attention. The mishap resulted in a big hue and cry, forcing the government to institute an inquiry. Your preliminary enquiry has revealed a series of anomalies. The material used for the construction was of poor quality. Despite the approved building plans permitting only one basement, an additional basement has been constructed. This was overlooked during the periodic inspections by the building inspector of the municipal corporation. In your enquiry, you noticed that the construction of the mall was given the green signal despite encroaching on areas earmarked for a green belt and a slip road in the Zonal Master Plan of the city. The permission to construct the mall was accorded by the previous Municipal Commissioner who is not only your senior and well known to you professionally, but also a good friend. Prima facie, the case appears to be of a widespread nexus between officials of the Municipal Corporation and the builders. Your colleagues are putting pressure on you to go slow in the enquiry. The builder, who is rich and influential, happens to be a close relative of a powerful minister in the state cabinet. The builder is persuading you to hush up the matter, promising you a fortune to do so. He also hinted that if this matter is not resolved at the earliest in his favour there is somebody in his office who is waiting to file a case against you under the POSH act.

**a. Discuss the ethical issues involved in the case.

**b. What are the options available to you in this situation?

**c. Explain your selected course of action.
This case presents an administrative and ethical crisis involving institutional corruption, structural negligence, safety failures, and direct threats to upright public oversight.
**Guiding Principle - &quot;Karmanye Vadhikaraste Ma Phaleshu Kadachana&quot; (Your duty is to perform your prescribed action, never to look for rewards or fear the consequences).
**Ethical Issues Involved

**1. Corruption and Abuse of Public Office - Nexus between builders and municipal officials for illegal approvals.

**2. Violation of Public Safety - Use of substandard material endangered human lives.

**3. Negligence in Regulatory Oversight - Failure of building inspections despite illegal additional basement.

**4. Exploitation of Labour - Employment of daily wage workers and minors in unsafe conditions.

**5. Conflict of Interest - Previous Municipal Commissioner being a friend and senior officer.

**6. Political Interference - Influence of minister through relation with builder compromises impartiality.

**7. Attempted Bribery - Builder offering financial inducement to suppress truth.

**8. Threat and Intimidation - Misuse of POSH provisions as a coercive weapon.

**9. Environmental Ethics Violation - Encroachment on green belt and slip road harms sustainable urban planning.

**10. Erosion of Public Trust - Citizens lose faith in institutions due to collusion and impunity.
**Options Available

1. Conduct a Transparent, Evidence-Backed Investigation and Enforce the Law: Exposes the full official-builder nexus, ensures immediate victim justice, and rejects all illegal threats.

2. Accept the Bribe and Quietly Hush Up the Inquiry: Compromises personal integrity completely, protects criminals, and fails the deceased victims.

3. Yield to Peer Pressure and Deliberately Slow Down the Investigation: Saves personal relations and career comfort but represents a surrender of administrative duty.

4. Resign from the Post to Avoid Professional Stress: Escapes personal accountability entirely while leaving systemic corruption and illegal structures unchecked.

5. Recuse from the Inquiry Citing Personal Conflict of Interest: Removes oneself due to friendship with the predecessor but delays immediate crisis resolution.

6. file a Diluted Report Sacrificing Only Lower-Level Field Staff: Protects senior bureaucratic friends and powerful builders while unfairly scapegoating lower-level inspectors.
**Selected Course of Action and Justification


**Course of Action
**Ethical Justification

Ensure immediate medical treatment and compensation for victims
Compassion, welfare of vulnerable workers

Seal construction site and stop further work
Prevent further danger and preserve evidence

Request **experts from premier institutions like the IITs to **conduct scientific checks on the structural strength and material quality.
**Secures objective scientific evidence that cannot be diluted by internal municipal corruption.

File FIR against builder, contractors, and responsible officials
Rule of law and deterrence

Document all proceedings and maintain transparency
Protects against political pressure and false allegations

Inform state vigilance/anti-corruption agencies about bribery attempt and intimidation
Moral courage and institutional accountability

Recommend systemic reforms in inspection, labour safety, and approval mechanisms
Preventive ethics and long-term governance reform

A civil servant’s true test lies not in routine administration but in standing firm during moral crises. By acting transparently, lawfully, and compassionately, the Municipal Commissioner can uphold public trust, ensure justice for victims, and reinforce the foundational democratic principle that no individual is above the law.
**2019
**

### Case Study 1 (Year: 2020)

In recent times, there has been an increasing concern in India to develop effective civil service ethics, code of conduct, transparency measures, ethics and integrity systems and anti-corruption agencies. In view of this, there is a need to focus on three specific areas, which are directly relevant to the problems of internalizing integrity and ethics in the civil services. These are as follows- 1. Anticipating specific threats to ethical standards and integrity in the civil services, 2. Strengthening the ethical competence of civil servants and 3. Developing administrative processes and practices which promote ethical values and integrity in civil services.

**a. **Suggest institutional measures to address the above three issues.
The **2nd ARC (4th Report - 'Ethics in Governance') observed that ethics in public life cannot be sustained merely by punitive law. It requires institutional design that prevents wrongdoing, builds character, and rewards integrity.
**Guiding Principle - &quot;Probity in Governance is the bedrock of democracy.&quot; (2nd ARC)
**Anticipating Specific Threats to Ethical Standards

1. Strengthen the Central Vigilance Commission (CVC) with statutory teeth and **predictive risk profiling of corruption-prone posts (revenue, transport, mining, procurement).

2. Operationalise an **integrated Integrity Index for every Ministry and Department, ranking institutions on transparency, complaint redressal, and audit compliance.

3. Mandatory Asset & Liability Disclosure under Section 44 of the Lokpal Act, 2013, with public dashboards for higher civil services.

4. Beneficial Ownership Registry to detect conflict-of-interest through shell entities and family-held assets.

5. Operationalise the **Whistle Blowers Protection Act, 2014 with anonymous, encrypted reporting channels and witness protection.

6. Lateral Vigilance Audits - random behavioural and decision-pattern audits, learning from the **UK's 'Nolan Principles' enforcement model.

7. Pre-emptive Conflict-of-Interest Declarations before sensitive postings (procurement, regulatory clearances, judicial postings).

8. Periodic Threat Assessment Reports, like the FATF's risk-based approach, for emerging corruption typologies (cyber-bribery, cryptocurrency kickbacks etc).
**Strengthening the Ethical Competence of Civil Servants

1. Foundation-Level Ethics Modules at LBSNAA, SVPNPA and central training institutes with case-based pedagogy, drawing from the Cadre Training Plan.

2. Mid-Career Mandatory Refreshers in ethics (Phase III, IV, V trainings) - proposed by **Baswan Committee (2016).

3. Code of Ethics for Civil Servants - distinct from the Conduct Rules - as recommended by **Hota Committee (2004) and 2nd ARC; aspirational, not merely prohibitive.

4. Ethics Officers in every Ministry/Department, drawn from senior civil servants of unblemished record, **for confidential guidance - modelled on the **US Office of Government Ethics.

5. Mentorship Programmes pairing new entrants with retired civil servants of integrity

6. Behavioural Competency Assessment as part of the 360-degree appraisal for empanelment under the Performance Appraisal Reports.

7. Compulsory study of foundational texts - Bhagavad Gita, Tirukkural, Arthashastra alongside Aristotle, Kant, Rawls - to root ethical reasoning in Indian philosophical traditions.

8. Ethics Helpline - a confidential advisory service for officers facing ethical dilemmas in real time.
**Administrative Processes Promoting Ethical Values

1. Citizen Charters & Service Delivery Guarantees with penalty clauses - operational in Madhya Pradesh, Bihar, Karnataka.

2. e-Governance & Process Re-engineering to eliminate discretion - DBT, PFMS, GeM portal have demonstrably reduced bribery touchpoints.

3. Social Audits as institutionalised in MGNREGA (Section 17) and PDS in Andhra Pradesh and Meghalaya.

4. RTI Strengthening with proactive disclosure under Section 4 and protection of RTI users.

5. Performance-linked Rewards - Eg- Civil Services Day Awards, PM's Excellence Awards.

6. Independent Civil Services Boards as directed in **T.S.R. Subramanian v. Union of India (2013) for transparent postings and minimum tenure.

7. Time-bound File Disposal & Digital Trail through e-Office for accountability and traceability.

8. Annual Ethics Audit - independent third-party review of departmental processes (akin to ISO certification).

9. Beneficiary Feedback Mechanism - **Jan-Sunwai, MyGov, social media grievance redressal closes the citizen-state loop.

10. Sevottam Framework for service quality and grievance redressal - institutionalising continuous improvement.
**Innovative Measures

1. Integrity Pacts in public procurement, monitored by Independent External Monitors (IEMs) - already in use by ONGC, GAIL, NTPC.

2. Behavioural Nudges - disclosure boxes, transparency walls, public display of pendency, as piloted by Indian Bureaucracy Lab.

3. Reputation Score for departments based on grievance disposal, audit objection clearance, and public trust surveys.
**&quot;The price of greatness is responsibility.&quot; - Winston Churchill
Transition from 'punishment-centric' to 'character-centric' governance is the surest path to probity in public life.
**

---

### Case Study 2 (Year: 2019)

An apparel manufacturing company having a large number of women employees was losing sales due to various factors. The company hired a reputed marketing executive, who increased the volume of sales within a short span of time. However, some unconfirmed reports came up regarding his indulgence in sexual harassment at the workplace. After sometime a women employee launched a formal complaint to the management against the marketing executive about sexually harassing her. Faced with the companies’ indifference, in not taking cognizance of her grievances, she lodged an FIR with the police. Realizing the sensitivity and gravity of the situation, the company called the women employee to negotiate. In that she was offered a hefty sum of money to withdraw the complaint and the FIR and also give in writing that the marketing executive is not involved in the case.

**a. Identify the ethical issues involved in this case.

**b. What options are available to the women employee?
Workplace sexual harassment violates dignity, equality, and safety, undermining ethical organisational culture and women’s constitutional rights.
**Guiding principle - **Deontological Ethics - Ends cannot justify the means Stakeholders

• The complainant woman employee.

• Other women employees (current and potential future victims).

• The accused marketing executive.

• Company management and shareholders.

• The Internal Committee under POSH Act, 2013.

• Society at large - message on workplace safety.
**Ethical Issues Involved

1. Violation of Bodily Integrity & Dignity - sexual harassment offends Article 21 (right to live with dignity) as upheld in Vishaka v. State of Rajasthan (1997).

2. Corporate Indifference to Statutory Duty - failure to constitute or activate the Internal Committee violates Section 4 of the POSH Act.

3. Commodification of Justice - offering money to withdraw a criminal complaint treats justice as a tradeable commodity.

4. Abuse of power & Suppression of Truth - coercing a false written statement that the executive 'was not involved' is fabrication of evidence, attracting Sections 227-229 of BNS, 2023.

5. Profit over People - sales performance is being shielded at the cost of women employees' dignity.

6. Power Asymmetry & Coercion - the complainant is financially vulnerable; the company exploits this to silence her.

7. Conflict between Individual Relief and Collective Justice - settlement protects one victim but exposes others.

8. Erosion of Workplace Trust - silence sends a signal that harassment is tolerated if the perpetrator is 'productive'.

9. Breach of Vicarious Responsibility - Section 19 of POSH Act mandates the employer to provide a safe environment.

10. Obstruction of Justice - interference with an ongoing FIR can amount to a separate criminal offence.
**Options Available to the Woman Employee

**1. Accept the Settlement and Withdraw the Complaint

•** Pros - immediate financial security; avoids prolonged legal battle and possible victimisation.

•** Cons - enables a serial offender; legally questionable since criminal proceedings under BNS for sexual offences cannot be compounded by private settlement.

**2. Reject the Offer and Pursue the Complaint to its Logical Conclusion

•** Pros - upholds rule of law; protects future victims; sets an institutional precedent.

•** Cons - possible workplace retaliation, social stigma, prolonged litigation, emotional toll.

**3. File a Formal Complaint with the Internal Committee under POSH Act, 2013

•** Pros - confidential, time-bound (90 days for inquiry), lower threshold of proof than criminal trial.

•** Cons - limited remedy (transfer, suspension, compensation); does not replace criminal prosecution.

**4. Approach the Local Committee (if no ICC) or She-Box Portal of the Ministry of Women and Child Development

• Pros - independent of employer pressure.

• Cons - may be slower; awareness limited.

**5. Seek Legal Counsel and NGO Support (e.g., Majlis, Lawyers Collective, NCW)

• Pros - informed decision-making; emotional and procedural support; protection from intimidation.

• Cons - none significant; this is a complementary step.
**Most Appropriate Course
**Combine Options 2, 3, 4 and 5 - pursue the FIR, simultaneously file a POSH complaint, approach the She-Box and NCW for institutional protection, and seek counselling/NGO support.
Refuse the settlement, since

a. Criminal sexual offences are non-compoundable and any 'settlement' has no legal force,

b. Silence emboldens future harassment

c. The integrity of the justice process cannot be auctioned.
Upholding dignity and justice is not merely a legal obligation but a moral imperative essential for creating safe and equitable workplaces.
**

---

### Case Study 3 (Year: 2019)

In one of the districts of a frontier state, narcotics menace has been rampant. This has resulted in money laundering, mushrooming of poppy farming, arms smuggling and near stalling of education. The system is on the verge of collapse. The situation has been further worsened by unconfirmed reports that local politicians, as well as some senior police officers, are providing surreptitious patronage to the drug mafia. At that point of time a woman police officer, known for her skills in handling such situations is appointed as superintendent of police to bring the situation to normalcy.

**a. If you are the same police officer, identify the various dimensions of the crisis.

**b. Based on your understanding, suggest measures to deal with the crisis.
This case mirrors the 'Udta Punjab' phenomenon, where the narco-economy has hollowed out institutions, communities and an entire generation.
**Guiding Principle - &quot;The duty of the police is not merely to enforce the law but to keep peace with justice.&quot; (Padmanabhaiah Committee, 2000)
**Ethical Principles Involved

**1. Rule of Law

**2. Integrity

**3. Public Service

**4. Courage of Conviction

**5. Compassion and Rehabilitation

**6. National Security Ethics
**Dimensions of the Crisis

**1. Law and Order Dimension - Expansion of organised crime networks, arms smuggling, and violent activities.

**2. National Security Dimension - Frontier location increases risk of cross-border narco-terrorism and illegal trafficking.

**3. Political-Criminal Nexus - Alleged patronage by politicians and senior police officers weakens governance legitimacy.

**4. Economic Dimension - Money laundering and illegal economy distort legitimate local businesses and taxation systems.

**5. Agrarian Crisis - Farmers shift to poppy cultivation due to poverty, debt, and high illegal profits.

**6. Educational Collapse - Addiction and criminalisation among youth lead to school dropouts and declining human capital.

**7. Public Health Crisis - Drug abuse causes mental illness, overdoses, HIV transmission, and family breakdown.

**8. Social and Ethical Breakdown - Fear, corruption, and criminal influence erode community trust and moral values.

**9. Administrative Corruption - Compromised officials obstruct fair policing and anti-drug operations.

**10. Gender Dimension - Women and children suffer domestic violence, trafficking, and economic insecurity due to addiction.
**Suggested Measures
**Immediate (0-3 months)

**1. Launch Special Anti-Narcotics Operations - Conduct intelligence-based raids against drug cartels, trafficking routes, and illegal warehouses.

**2. Target Financial Networks - Coordinate with Enforcement Directorate and financial intelligence units to freeze assets and investigate money laundering.

**3. Strengthen Border Surveillance - Increase coordination with Border Security Force and use drones, sensors, and checkpoints.

**4. Action against corrupt officials - Remove compromised officers from sensitive postings and initiate vigilance inquiries.

**5. Drug De-Addiction and Emergency Health Support - Establish temporary rehabilitation camps and counselling centres.

**6. Protection for Whistleblowers and Witnesses - Encourage citizens to report drug activities without fear of retaliation.
**Long-term measures

1. Engage NGOs (e.g., SPYM, Society for Promotion of Youth and Masses) and faith-based **organisations for community sensitisation.

2. Drug Free Campus campaign in schools and colleges with parents, teachers and student councils.

3. Open de-addiction and rehabilitation centres under the National Action Plan for Drug Demand Reduction (NAPDDR).

4. Alternative livelihood - link poppy farmers to MGNREGA, PMKSY, FPO formation, horticulture, ODOP.

5. Community policing - Panchayat-level Drug Watch Committees with women, youth, ASHA workers.

6. Data-driven enforcement - integrated drug-crime dashboard with predictive analytics.

7. Skill development through PMKVY for rescued youth; livelihood with dignity is the long-run antidote.

8. Education revival - reopen schools, ensure mid-day meals, teacher attendance, scholarships.
**Personal Conduct of the SP

1. Lead from the front - be visible at raids, hospitals, schools and de-addiction camps.

2. Maintain meticulous documentation; communicate with the DGP and CM's Office in writing on all sensitive decisions.

3. Use the media judiciously - transparency is the best armour against political pressure.

4. Refuse all extra-legal directions; record them; escalate to NHRC/CVC if necessary.

5. Personal security protocol - vary routes, secure family, use trusted PSOs.
A courageous and ethical police leadership must combine strict law enforcement with compassion, institutional reform, and community participation.
**

---

### Case Study 4 (Year: 2019)

Honesty and uprightness are the hallmarks of a civil servant. Civil servants possessing these qualities are considered as the backbone of any strong organisation. In line of duty, they take various decisions, at times some become bonafide mistakes. As long as such decisions are not taken intentionally and do not benefit personally, the officer cannot be said to be guilty. Though such decisions may, at times, lead to unforeseen adverse consequences in the long-term. In the recent past, a few instances have surfaced wherein civil servants have been implicated for bonafide mistakes. They have often been prosecuted and even imprisoned. These instances have greatly rattled the moral fibre of the civil servants.

**a. How does this trend affect the functioning of the civil services?

**b. What measures can be taken to ensure that honest civil servants are not implicated for bonafide mistakes on their part? Justify your answer.
&quot;The civil service is not a service of status, but a service of responsibility. It is a commitment to the conscience of the nation.&quot; - **Sardar Vallabhbhai Patel
Sardar Patel envisioned the civil services as the **&quot;Steel Frame&quot; of India. He held that this frame must be strong enough to withstand political pressure and independent enough to offer impartial advice. However, when honest officers are criminalized for **bonafide mistakes the steel frame begins to rust.
**Impact of this trend on functioning of civil service

1. Decision Paralysis - Officers delay files and avoid taking responsibility due to fear of future prosecution.

2. Risk-Averse Bureaucracy - Preference for **status quo over innovation and reform-oriented decisions.

3. Erosion of Moral Courage - Officers hesitate to take bold decisions even when public interest demands it.

4. Procedural Over-Compliance - Excessive rule-following replaces **outcome-oriented governance.

5. Decline in Public Service Motivation - Honest officers feel **demoralised and insecure.

6. Blame-Shifting Culture - Decisions are pushed upwards or sideways to avoid personal accountability.

7. Project Delays and Cost Overruns - Infrastructure and welfare projects suffer due to administrative hesitation.

8. Loss of Administrative Talent - Officers seek “safe” postings or opt for premature retirement.

9. Weakening of Ethical Climate - Fear replaces **values-based decision-making.

10. Trust Deficit in Institutions - Strained relations between civil servants, political executive, and investigative agencies.

11. Erosion of public faith in administration
**Measures to protect honest civil servants from implication in bonafide mistakes

1. Ensuring **autonomy of civil servants by establishing **civil services boards for transparent postings and transfers. It can prevent **politicization of bureaucracy

2. Establishing clear **Code of ethics as suggested by 2nd ARC

3. Enhancing **public participation in decision making. Eg- Kerala’s people’s plan campaign

4. Clear Legal Distinction Between Error and Corruption- Criminal liability should arise only when **mens rea or personal gain is established.

5. Mandatory Prior Sanction for Prosecution- Acts as a **protective filter against frivolous or motivated cases.

6. Expert Advisory Committees: Pre-investigation review by retired bureaucrats/domain experts.

7. Statute of Limitations: Setting a time limit (5-7 years) for reopening administrative files to prevent &quot;witch-hunts&quot;

8. Time-Bound Investigation and Trial- Prevents prolonged harassment and psychological pressure on officers.

9. Strengthening Documentation and Reasoned Orders- Well-recorded rationale demonstrates **absence of malafide intent.

10. Clear Guidelines on Use of Discretion- Reduces ambiguity and arbitrary interpretation of administrative actions.

11. Ethics and Risk-Assessment Training- Helps officers anticipate consequences and take **ethically defensible decisions.

12. Training for Investigating Agencies: Sensitizing the CBI/CVC/CAG on administrative realities.
**Innovative methods

**1. Professional Indemnity Insurance (PII) - If an officer is sued for a decision taken in their official capacity, the insurance covers legal costs.

**2. Business Judgment Rule (USA & Germany) - Unless &quot;gross negligence&quot; or &quot;bad faith&quot; is proven, the official is immune from personal liability.
The strength of a democracy lies in the **courage of its public servants. While there must be zero tolerance for corruption, there must be **maximum protection for conviction. This requires an &quot;Intelligent Accountability&quot; framework that encourages officers to be bold, innovative, and decisive.
**

---

### Case Study 5 (Year: 2019)

You are heading the rescue operations in an area affected by severe natural calamity, thousands of people are rendered homeless and deprived of food, drinking water and other basic amenities. Rescue work has been disrupted by heavy rainfall and damage to supply routes. The local people are seeding with anger against the delayed limited rescue operations. When your team reaches the affected area, the people there heckle and even assault some of the team members. One of your team members is even severely injured. Faced with this crisis some team member pleads with you to call off the operations freeing threats to their life.

**a. In such trying circumstances, what will be your response?

**b. Examine the qualities of a public servant which will be required to manage the situations.
Natural disasters test not only administrative capacity but also the ethical commitment, courage, and compassion of public servants.
**Guiding Principle - Seva Paramo Dharma (Service is the highest duty).
**My Response

1. Evacuate and provide medical care to the injured team member -life of the responder is non-negotiable.

2. Reassure the team - call a brief huddle;
acknowledge their fear; affirm that abandoning the affected is not an option.

3. Refuse to call off operations - this would be (i) a dereliction of duty, (ii) a precedent inviting violence as a coercive


tool, and (iii) catastrophic
for the thousands still trapped.

4. Empathic engagement - meet the angry community leaders directly, listen without defensiveness.

5. Transparent communication - clearly explain the constraints (rainfall, blocked routes, equipment limits) and the action plan, with timelines.

6. Engage community leaders, sarpanchs, religious heads, school teachers - co-opt the angry crowd into the rescue effort.

7. Restructure operations - reorganise into smaller, dispersed teams escorted by local volunteers and police, prioritising the most vulnerable (children, elderly, pregnant women).

8. Coordinate with district administration for relief camps, dry rations, drinking water, mobile medical units.

9. Request reinforcements - call the NDRF, SDRF, paramilitary and Indian Army (HADR - Humanitarian
Assistance & Disaster Relief) under the Disaster Management Act, 2005.

10. File an FIR against the assailants once the rescue stabilises.

11. Single-window grievance redressal with a dedicated officer and toll-free helpline.

12. Counselling for traumatised survivors and for the rescue team itself.
**Qualities of a Public Servant Required

1. Moral Courage - the will to act rightly despite personal risk. Mahatma Gandhi called this the foundation of all virtues.

2. Empathy & Compassion - the ability to feel the survivor's grief and respond with care .

3. Emotional Intelligence - recognising one's own fear and the team's anxiety; converting fear into focus.

4. Equanimity (Sthitaprajna) - composure amidst chaos.

5. Leadership by Example - the leader must be physically present at the most dangerous point of operations.

6. Communication Skills - the ability to listen, explain, persuade and de-escalate.

7. Resilience & Stamina - physical, mental and ethical endurance over days of crisis.

8. Decisiveness - ability to take timely, decisive and bold decisions..

9. Team Spirit & Care for Subordinates - a leader who protects the team commands greater obedience than one who orders it.

10. Constitutional Morality - placing duty to citizens above hierarchical loyalty or self-preservation.

11. Risk Management - calibrated courage, not recklessness; protecting the team while serving the public.

12. Foresight - anticipating bottlenecks (logistics, communication, secondary disasters).
**Real-World Parallels

• IPS officer Vinay Tiwari leading rescue in the 2011 Sikkim earthquake despite hostile terrain and crowds.

• Air Commodore Rajesh Isser commanding 'Operation Rahat' during the 2013 Kedarnath floods - over 23,000 evacuated.
&quot;In the moment of crisis, the wise person builds bridges; the fool builds barriers.&quot; - **Nyaya Sutra
A public servant in such a situation must remember that hostility is the language of grief. To answer grief with courage and compassion is the highest form of public service.
**

---

### Case Study 6 (Year: 2019)

In a modern democratic polity, there is a concept of the political executive and permanent executive. Elected people’s representatives form the political executive and bureaucracy form the permanent executive. Ministers frame policy decisions and bureaucrats execute these. In the initial decades after independence, the relationship between the permanent executives and the political executives were characterized by mutual understanding, respect, and cooperation, without encroaching upon each other’s domain. However, in subsequent decades, the situation has changed. There are instances of the political executive insisting upon the permanent executives to follow its agenda. Respect for an appreciation of upright bureaucrats has declined. There is an increasing tendency among the political executive to get involved in routine administrative matters such as transfers, posting etc. Under this scenario, there is a definitive trend towards ‘politicization of bureaucracy’. The rising materialism and acquisitiveness in social life has also adversely impacted upon the ethical values of both the permanent executive and the political executive.

**a. **What are the consequences of this ‘politicization of bureaucracy? Discuss.
**Sardar Patel described the civil services as the **'steel frame' that holds India together. Articles 309-311 sought to insulate civil servants from political vagaries.
However, the **Hota Committee (2004), the 2nd ARC (10th Report) etc have sounded the alarm on the corrosion of this frame through politicisation.
**Consequences of Politicisation of Bureaucracy
Politicization of bureaucracy refers to a situation where civil servants act based on **political loyalty, partisan interests, or pressure from political executives rather than constitutional values, neutrality, professionalism, and public interest.

**1.

**Administrative Consequences

• **Distorted Policy Priorities - schemes driven by electoral calculus (loan waivers, sub-quotas) over developmental rationality.

THE WHEEL OF CORRUPTION


•


•


•


•


•


**Decision Paralysis - officers hesitate to take bold decisions, fearing punitive transfers.
**Frequent Transfers - undermining institutional memory and continuity of programmes; average tenure of an IAS officer in some states is under a year. **Erosion of Hierarchy - junior officers bypass seniors using political channels. **Reduced Quality of Service Delivery -particularly to weaker sections without political patronage.
**Substitution of Merit with Loyalty -postings driven by allegiance, not



competence.

•

**Demoralisation of Honest Officers - talented officers seek deputation, foreign assignments, or premature retirement.

•** Risk Aversion - preference for status quo over reform; the 'safe file' culture.

**2.

**Ethical & Moral Consequences

•** Loss of Anonymity & Neutrality

•** Compromise of Integrity - officers willing to bend reward themselves; honest ones marginalised.

•** Normalisation of Corruption - quid pro quo postings monetise public office.

•** Crisis of Conscience for Young Entrants - IAS/IPS probationers learn early that integrity has institutional costs.

•** Erosion of Professional Pride - civil service identity gives way to political identity.

**3. Institutional Consequences

•** Politicised Investigation Agencies - when CBI, ED, IT or police are perceived as partisan, public trust collapses (the 'caged parrot' observation by the Supreme Court).

•** Federalism Strain - All-India Services officers caught between Centre-State conflicts.

•** Decline in Public Trust - citizens see civil servants as agents of ruling parties, not of the
Constitution.

•** Crony Capitalism Risk - politicised regulators favour connected firms.

**4. Constitutional & Legal Consequences

•** Breach of All-India Services (Conduct) Rules - political activity by civil servants prohibited but often blurred.

•** Negation of T.S.R. Subramanian directions - minimum tenure, Civil Services Boards, written instructions seldom honoured.

**5. Internal Civil Service Culture

•** Factionalism - officers cluster around political camps.

•** Erosion of Esprit de Corps - solidarity replaced by individual survival strategies.

•** Brain Drain - best minds choose international organisations or private sector.

•** Ashok Khemka, IAS - over 50 transfers in 30 years for resisting irregular land transactions.

•** Durga Shakti Nagpal - suspended for cracking down on sand mafia.

•** Sanjiv Chaturvedi - fought corruption at AIIMS despite repeated punitive transfers;
awarded Magsaysay.

•** U. Sagayam, IAS - punitive transfers for exposing granite mafia in Tamil Nadu.
**Way Forward

**1. Operationalise Civil Services Boards (per T.S.R. Subramanian, 2013).

**2. Minimum tenure of 2 years for sensitive posts.

**3. Written instructions for all sensitive decisions (Hota Committee).

**4. Independent Civil Services Authority for All-India Services.

**5. Strengthening Conduct Rules and Code of Ethics.

**6. Periodic 360-degree appraisal with civil-society input.

7. Reforming the political class - internal democracy in parties, decriminalisation of politics. &quot;When the political executive treats the bureaucracy as a tool of governance rather than as a partner in governance, both suffer.&quot; - 2nd ARC
**2018
**

### Case Study 1 (Year: 2019)

A big corporate house is engaged in manufacturing industrial chemicals on a large scale. It proposes to set upon the additional unit. Many states rejected its proposal due to the detrimental effect on the environment. But one state government acceded to the request and permitted the unit close to a city, brushing aside all opposition. The unit was set up 10 years ago and was in full swing till recently. The pollution caused by the industrial effluents was affecting the land, water and crops in the area. It was also causing serious health problems to human beings and animals. This gave rise to a series of agitation thousands of people took part, creating a law and order problem necessitating stern police action. Following the public outcry, the State government ordered the closure of the factory. The closure of the factory resulted in the unemployment of not only those workers who were engaged in the factory but also those who were working in the ancillary units. It also very badly affected those industries which depended on the chemicals manufactured by it.

**a. **As a senior officer entrusted with the responsibility of handling these issues, how are you going to address it?
This case mirrors the Bhopal Gas Tragedy aftermath, the Sterlite Copper closure in Tuticorin (2018) and the Vedanta-Niyamgiri controversy. As the senior officer, I face a classic ethical dilemma - environmental protection vs livelihood security
**Stakeholders

1. Local community - health, environment, drinking water, agricultural livelihoods.

2. Factory workers - direct employment.

3. Ancillary unit workers and dependent industries - derived livelihoods.

4. Corporate house - investment, reputation.

5. State Government - revenue, political stability.

6. Environmental ecosystem - soil, groundwater, biodiversity.

7. Future generations - inter-generational equity.
**Guiding Principle - Sustainable Development (Brundtland, 1987) and the Polluter-Pays Principle. Ethical Issues Involved

1. Right to Life & Healthy Environment - Article 21 (Subhash Kumar v. State of Bihar, 1991).

2. Right to Livelihood - Article 21 read with 39(a) (Olga Tellis v. BMC, 1985).

3. Inter-generational Equity - present prosperity vs future ecological inheritance.

4. Regulatory Failure - pollution boards either lacked capacity or were captured.

5. Corporate Social Responsibility - the principle of trusteeship (Gandhiji).

6. Distributive Justice - who bears the cost of industrialisation?

7. Environmental Justice - ecologically poor communities disproportionately harmed.

8. Procedural Ethics - EIA, public hearings, consent - were they conducted in spirit?
**My Course of Action
**Immediate (0–30 days)

1. Independent Health & Environmental Audit by NEERI, IIT or AIIMS - pollutant levels, soil and water contamination, disease burden.

2. Emergency medical camps - free testing, treatment and long-term monitoring for affected residents and livestock.

3. Alternative drinking water supply via tankers, reverse-osmosis units, deep tube-wells in unaffected aquifers.

4. Interim relief & compensation under the Public Liability Insurance Act, 1991 and 'Polluter Pays' doctrine - funded by the company, not the taxpayer.

5. Wage support for workers - emergency relief through state labour welfare board, ESIC.
**Short-Term (1–6 months)

1. Show-Cause to Company - formal proceedings under Water (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1974, Air Act, 1981, EPA, 1986 and Hazardous Wastes Rules, 2016.

2. Two-Track Approach

•** Track 1 - Conditional Reopening - only after the company invests in zero-liquid discharge, scrubbers, real-time emission monitoring, third-party certification, and an environmental bank guarantee.

•** Track 2 - if pollution is irremediable, order complete closure.

3. National Green Tribunal - refer the matter to NGT for an independent expert determination.

4. Worker Re-skilling - Pradhan Mantri Kaushal Vikas Yojana (PMKVY), DDU-GKY for ancillary unit workers.

5. Bridge employment - absorb skilled workers in nearby industrial corridors, MSMEs, MGNREGA for unskilled.

6. Special Economic Package for the region - incentives for clean industries (electronics, food processing, solar) to set up nearby.
**Long-Term

1. Strengthen Pollution Control Board - additional staff, equipment, autonomy, transparent grievance portal.

2. Ecological Restoration - bioremediation, phytoremediation, afforestation; cost borne by the company under 'Polluter Pays'.

3. Long-term Health Surveillance - cohort study for 10 years to track delayed effects (cancer, kidney, respiratory).

4. Diversified Industrial Base - encourage green industries, agro-processing, IT-enabled services.

5. Strengthening EIA Regime - independent EIA consultants, public participation, social impact assessment.

6. Industrial Township Reform - green belts, buffer zones, evacuation drills, emergency response.

7. Just Transition Fund - modelled on the European Union's mechanism for fossil-fuel-dependent regions.
**Real-World Parallels

1. Sterlite Copper, Tuticorin (Tamil Nadu, 2018) - closure upheld by Supreme Court (2024).

2. Bhopal Gas Tragedy aftermath - failure of just transition continues to haunt three generations.

3. Bichhri Village (Rajasthan) - Supreme Court ordered the polluter to bear full cost of remediation.

4. Plachimada Coca-Cola Plant (Kerala) - closure linked to groundwater depletion.
&quot;Earth provides enough to satisfy every man's needs, but not every man's greed.&quot; - **Mahatma Gandhi
As a senior officer, my duty is to ensure that development serves humanity, not the other way around.
**

---

### Case Study 2 (Year: 2018)

It is a State where prohibition is in force. You are recently appointed as the Superintendent of Police of a district notorious for illicit distillation of liquor. The illicit liquor leads to many death, reported and unreported, and causes a major problem for the district authorities. The approach till now had been to view it as a law and order problem and tackle it accordingly. Raids, arrest, police cases, and criminal trials - all these had only limited impact. The problem remains as serious as ever. Your inspections show that the parts of the district where the distillation flourishes are economically, industrially and educationally backward. Agriculture is badly affected by poor irrigation facilities. Frequent clashes among communities gave boost to illicit distillation. No major initiatives had taken place in the past either from the government’s side or from social organizations to improve the lot of the people.

**a. **Which new approach will you adopt to bring the problem under control?”
The case parallels **Bihar's prohibition experience, and 2015 Malwani hooch tragedy in Maharashtra. It demands a fundamental shift from 'enforcement-only' to a 'development-policing' integrated model.
**Operational Doctrine - Police as Catalysts of Development, not just Enforcers of Law (2nd ARC, 5th Report)
**Why the Old Approach Failed

1. Treated symptom (distillation) not the disease (poverty, joblessness, social fragmentation).

2. Police-centric - ignored education, livelihood and gender dimensions.

**3. Adversarial - community saw police as the enemy, distillers as benefactors.

4. No long-term substitute economy - every arrested distiller was replaced by another.

5. Demand-side neglect - consumers continued, and the supply chain regenerated.

6. Weak inter-departmental coordination - Excise, Health, Education, Rural Development worked in silos.
**My New Approach -
**Pillar 1 - Intelligence-Driven Enforcement

1. Mapping - GIS-based mapping of distillation hotspots, supply routes, financiers and consumption clusters.

2. Choke the Supply Chain - focus on financiers, large-scale brewers and wholesale suppliers, not petty vendors.

3. Asset Attribution & Seizure under PMLA and state Excise Acts - financial deterrent is sharper than imprisonment.

4. Beat-Level Constable Engagement - community-friendly, beat-bonded officers who know every household.

5. Coordinated Action - joint operations with Excise, GST Intelligence, Forest Department (for raw material sourcing) and SDRF.

6. Withdraw Petty Cases - selectively decriminalise small-time consumers; channel them to de-addiction. **Pillar 2 - Livelihood Substitution

1. Convene a District Development Coordination Meeting with the Collector - present the diagnosis and seek convergence.

2. MGNREGA Push - focus on water-harvesting structures, check dams, farm ponds to address the irrigation deficit identified.

3. Skill Development under PMKVY, DDU-GKY for youth - masonry, plumbing, electrical, IT, hospitality.

4. One District One Product (ODOP) - identify a niche product (e.g., honey, lac, minor forest produce) for branding and market linkage.
**Pillar 3 - Education & Social Reform

1. Re-energise Sarva Shiksha Abhiyan and Samagra Shiksha - focus on school attendance, mid-day meals, female literacy.

2. Skill Schools - vocational training partnerships with ITIs and Polytechnics.

3. Reconciliation Camps for inter-community clashes - invoke Gram Nyayalayas, traditional panchayats and SP-led 'Aman Committees'.
**Pillar 4 - De-addiction & Health

1. Integrated Rehabilitation Centres for Addicts (IRCAs) under NAPDDR, MSJE.

2. Mobile De-addiction Units in remote pockets.

3. ASHA & ANM Sensitisation - community-level identification and referral.
**Pillar 5 - Community Ownership

1. Gram Sabha Resolutions declaring villages liquor-free - moral pressure beats moralising lectures.

2. Mahila Mandals as guardians of village ethics - **Andhra Pradesh's anti-arrack movement (early 1990s) was led by rural women.

3. Youth Clubs / Nehru Yuva Kendra Sangathan for awareness, sports, employment camps.

4. Religious Leaders & Local Influencers - engage them as moral campaigners.

5. Reward & Recognition - annually felicitate liquor-free villages and individual reformers.

6. Citizen Charter for Excise & Police - transparent grievance redressal.
**Innovative Tools

1. Predictive Policing - data analytics to forecast brewing locations during festival seasons.

2. Drone Surveillance over inaccessible forested distillation zones.

3. Public–Private Partnerships with CSR funds of nearby industries for skill centres and clinics.

4. Whistleblower Reward Scheme - anonymous reporting with cash awards.
An **ethical and effective Superintendent of Police must combine strict enforcement with **empathy, community participation, development initiatives, and rehabilitation. Such a holistic approach transforms policing from a force of fear into an **instrument of social justice and human welfare.
**

---

### Case Study 3 (Year: 2018)

Dr X is a leading medical practitioner in a city. He has set up a charitable trust through which he plans to establish a super-speciality hospital in the city to cater to the medical needs of all sections of the society. Incidentally, that part of the State had been neglected over the years. The proposed hospital would be a boon for the region. You are heading the tax investigation agency of that region. During an inspection of the doctor’s clinic, your officers have found some major irregularities. A few of them are substantial which had resulted in considerable withholding of tax that should be paid by him now. The doctor is cooperative. He undertakes to pay the tax immediately. However, there are certain other deficiencies in his tax compliance which are purely technical in nature. If these technical defaults are pursued by the agency, considerable time and energy of the doctor will be diverted to issues which are not so serious, urgent or even helpful to the tax collection process. Further, in all probability, it will hamper the prospects of the hospital coming up. There are two options before you-

**1. Taking a broader view, ensure substantial tax compliance and ignore defaults that are merely technical in nature.

**2. Pursue the matter strictly and proceed on all fronts,whether substantial or merely technical.

**a. **As the head of the tax agency, which course of action will you opt and why?
As the head of the tax investigation agency, I am faced with an administrative dilemma of **Strict Legalism vs. Purposeful Governance. While the law demands absolute compliance, the spirit of public service requires an assessment of the &quot;greater good.&quot;
**Stakeholders and Their Interests

1. Citizens in neglected region

2. Dr. X

3. The State/Tax Administration

4. Wider Society
**Guiding principle - Sarve Bhavantu Sukhinah - May all beings be happy.
**Evaluation of the Two Options
**Option 1: Ensure Substantial Compliance and Ignore Merely Technical Defaults
**Merits

1. Public Interest Maximisation - Facilitates establishment of a **super-speciality charitable hospital, benefiting underserved populations.

2. Proportionality - Focuses enforcement on **material tax evasion, not procedural lapses.

3. Administrative Efficiency - Saves scarce investigative resources for **serious violations.

4. Encourages Voluntary Compliance - Doctor’s cooperation and immediate payment indicate **good faith.

5. Utilitarian Justification - Greater overall societal benefit with minimal ethical cost.
**Concerns

• Risk of **perceived leniency.

• Possibility of **precedent misuse if discretion is not well documented.
**Option 2: Strict Action on Both Substantial and Technical Defaults
**Merits

1. Rule-Based Equality - Upholds **formal equality before law.

2. Deterrence Value - Signals zero tolerance for any non-compliance.
**Demerits

1. Disproportionate Response - Treats minor technical lapses on par with substantive evasion.

2. Public Interest Harm - Likely delay or derailment of a **critical healthcare project.

3. Inefficient Governance - High administrative cost with low revenue gain.

4. Ethical Myopia - Focus on procedure over outcomes.
**I Would Opt for Option 1 as it leads to “greatest happiness of greatest number”
**Ethical Justification for the Chosen Option

1. In line with doctrine of proportionality - Punishment and enforcement correspond to **gravity of offence.

2. Intent-Based Ethics - technical lapses lack **malafide intent.

3. Public Welfare Priority - Health infrastructure in a neglected region serves **constitutional goals under Article 47.

4. Trusteeship Model of Governance - State acts as a **facilitator of social good, not an obstacle.

5. Utilitarian Ethics - Maximises net social benefit without compromising revenue.

6. Administrative Discretion within Law - Tax laws allow **compounding, warnings, and rectification windows.

7. Moral Legitimacy of the State - Citizens perceive the State as **fair and humane, not mechanical.

8. Encouragement of Philanthropy - Signals that ethical, cooperative taxpayers are treated reasonably.

9. Fulfills core mandate - Revenue collection, not procedural perfection, is the agency’s primary objective.
**Further safeguards

• Record a **reasoned written order.

• Ensure **full recovery of substantial dues.

• Issue **time-bound compliance directions for technical lapses.

• Apply **uniform standards in similar cases.
Ethical governance lies in **wise discretion, not rigid formalism. By choosing option 2, I can uphold **Dharma, Nyāya, and Lokasaṅgraha (public welfare)
**

---

### Case Study 4 (Year: 2018)

Rakesh is a responsible district level officer, who enjoys the trust of his higher officials. Knowing his honesty, the government entrusted him with the responsibility of identifying the beneficiaries under a health care scheme meant for senior citizens. The criteria to be a beneficiary are the following- 1. 60 years of age or above. 2. Belonging to a reserved community. 3. Family income of less than 1 Lakh rupees per annum. 4.
**Post-treatment prognosis is likely to be high to make a positive difference to the quality of life of the beneficiary. One day, an old couple visited Rakesh’s office with their application. They have been the residents of a village in his district since their birth. The old man is diagnosed with a rare condition that causes obstruction in the large intestine. As a consequence, he has severe abdominal pain frequently that prevents him from doing any physical labour. The couple has no children to support them. The expert surgeon whom they contacted is willing to do the surgery without charging any fee. However, the couple will have to bear the cost of incidental charges, such as medicines, hospitalization, etc., to the tune of rupees one lakh. The couple fulfils all the criteria except criterion ‘b’. However, any financial aid would certainly make a significant difference in their quality of life.

**a. **How should Rakesh respond to the situation?
Rakesh is caught between the letter of the rule (rigid eligibility) and the spirit of the rule (welfare of vulnerable senior citizens). This is a classic Niti vs Nyaya dilemma - Amartya Sen's distinction between procedural justice and substantive justice.
**Stakeholders

1. The elderly couple (immediate beneficiaries).

2. Other potential beneficiaries from the reserved community (precedential impact).

3. The State / Scheme administrators.

4. The wider taxpaying public.

5. Rakesh himself - career, conscience.
**Guiding Principle - &quot;Antyodaya&quot; (welfare of the last person)
**Ethical Issues Involved

1. Rule of Law vs Substantive Justice - strict compliance vs welfare outcome.

2. Compassion vs Equality - emotion vs equality before law (Article 14).

3. Discretion vs Discipline - extent of bureaucratic discretion.

4. Public Trust vs Personal Risk - favouring one couple may invite charges of arbitrariness.

5. Distributive Justice - diverting scheme funds may deny equally deserving reserved-community beneficiaries.

6. Dignity of the Elderly - Article 41 obligates state assistance to the aged and infirm.

7. Moral Hazard - informal exception-making may corrode rule-based governance.
**Options Available to Rakesh

**1. Sanction Aid by Stretching the Rules

**2. Strict Rejection - Strict Compliance

**3. Reject under this Scheme but Mobilise Alternative Resources

**4. Personal Charity (Rakesh pays from own pocket)
**Most Appropriate Course - Options 3
**Immediate Action (within days)

1. Politely explain to the couple that this specific scheme cannot cover them due to criterion (b), without dismissing their plight.

2. Map alternative resources promptly:

a. Rashtriya Arogya Nidhi (RAN) / Health Minister's Discretionary Grant - for catastrophic illness of BPL patients.

b. State-level Chief Minister's Relief Fund.

c. District Mineral Foundation Fund / DM's Discretionary Welfare Fund (where applicable).

d. CSR funds of local industries / hospital trusts / Lions/Rotary clubs.

e. Crowdfunding platforms (Milaap, Ketto) with district administration endorsement

3. Coordinate with the surgeon - explore further concessions on hospitalisation and medicines.

4. Mobilise Community and CSR Support - Coordinate with NGOs, charitable trusts, philanthropists, CSR initiatives, or local donors for incidental expenses.

5. Connect the couple with a **District Social Welfare Officer who can navigate paperwork for elderly applicants.

6. Track progress personally - assign a designated official to ensure follow-through as the couple has no children to advocate for them.
**Systemic Action
1. **Document the case in a written note to the District Collector and State Department, recommending **review of the eligibility criteria to include all economically and medically vulnerable senior citizens.

7. Propose a **'general category EWS senior citizen' sub-component within the scheme.

8. Suggest a **'Discretionary Reserve' (10% of scheme funds) for hardship cases under written, recorded justification.

9. Maintain a register of similar applicants denied on technicalities - evidence for future reform.
Such **balanced action reflects the essence of ethical governance - combining legality with humanity and procedure with empathy.
**

---

### Case Study 5 (Year: 2018)

As a senior officer in the Ministry, you have access to important policy decisions and upcoming big announcements such as road constructions projects before they are notified in the public domain. The Ministry is about to announce a mega road project for which the drawings are already in place. Sufficient care was taken by the planners to make use of the government land with the minimum land acquisition from private parties. Compensation rate for private parties was also finalized as per government rules. Care was also taken to minimize deforestation. Once the project is announced, it is expected that there will be a huge spurt in real estate prices in and around that area. Meanwhile, the Minister concerned insists that you realign the road in such a way that it comes closer to his 20 acres farmhouse. He also suggests that he would facilitate the purchase of a big plot of land in your wife name at the prevailing rate which is very nominal, in and around the proposed mega road project. He also tries to convince you by saying that there is no harm in it as he is buying the land legally. He even promises to supplement your savings in case you do not have sufficient funds to buy the land. However, by the act of realignment, a lot of agricultural lands has to be acquired, thereby causing a considerable financial burden on the government, and also the displacement of the farmers. As if this is not enough, it will involve cutting down of a large number of trees denuding the area of its green cover.

**a. Faced with this situation, what will you do?

**b. Critically examine various conflicts of interest and explain what your responsibilities are as a public servant.”
This case echoes the **Adarsh Society scam and the Karnataka mining cases - situations where insider information and political position created irresistible private-gain opportunities, with public cost.
**Stakeholders

Transparency and fairness.

**Various Conflicts of Interest Involved

**1. Public Interest vs Private Gain - Road alignment is sought not for public utility but for enhancing value of Minister’s farmhouse.

**2. Official Duty vs Political Pressure - Officer is expected to obey lawful authority but resist unethical interference.

**3. Integrity vs Personal Financial Benefit - Offer of land in wife’s name and financial assistance amounts to inducement and corruption.

**4. Transparency vs Insider Information Misuse - Using confidential project information for land purchase amounts to abuse of privileged information.

**5. Development vs Environmental Sustainability - Realignment would increase deforestation and ecological degradation.

**6. Public Exchequer vs Political Patronage - Additional land acquisition would impose unnecessary burden on taxpayers.

**7. Rights of Farmers vs Elite Interests - Farmers would face displacement for private enrichment of influential individuals.

**8. Professional Ethics vs Fear of Reprisal - Officer may face transfers, harassment, or political retaliation for resisting pressure.
**Responsibilities as a Public Servant

**1. Uphold Constitution and Rule of Law - Loyalty must be toward constitutional values, not individuals.

**2. Act in Public Interest - Infrastructure decisions should maximize social welfare and minimize harm.

**3. Maintain Integrity and Probity - Avoid corruption, nepotism, and conflict of interest.

**4. Protect Public Resources - Prevent unnecessary expenditure and misuse of taxpayers’ money.

**5. Ensure Environmental Stewardship - Development should align with sustainability principles.

**6. Remain Politically Neutral - Bureaucratic decisions must be impartial and evidence-based.

**7. Maintain Confidentiality - Sensitive information should not be used for personal enrichment.

**8. Demonstrate Moral Courage - resisting powerful interests. Eg- Ashok Khemka resisting politically influenced land dealings
&quot;In matters of style, swim with the current; in matters of principle, stand like a rock.&quot; - Thomas Jefferson
By remaining impartial, transparent, and constitutionally committed, a public servant preserves both democratic accountability and the moral legitimacy of the state.
